A line in the plane is called \\emph{sunny} if it is not parallel to any of the $x$-axis, the $y$-axis, or the line $x+y=0$.

Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.
\end{itemize}

Alternatively, we can try to construct a configuration with k=2 directly. For n=3, we tried k=2 and failed. For n=4,5,6 we tried and failed. But maybe for larger n, k=2 becomes possible. Let's analyze the general problem.

We have n lines. The points are all (a,b) with a,b >= 1, a+b <= n+1. Total points = n(n+1)/2. We need to cover these points with n lines. The lines can be of three types: horizontal (y = c), vertical (x = c), and slope -1 (x+y = s). Sunny lines are those not of these three types. So they have slopes not 0, undefined, -1. We need to determine all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points.

Observation: The points form a triangular grid. The set of all slope -1 lines (the diagonals) covering sums 2,3,...,n+1 gives n lines that cover all points. So k=0 is always possible.

Now, can we have k=1? That would be one sunny line and n-1 non-sunny lines. We need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover most points, and the sunny line covers the rest. For n=3,4,5,6 we found constructions. For general n, we can try to use the diagonals: take all slope -1 lines except one, say omit x+y = n+1, and then use a sunny line to cover the points with sum = n+1. But the points with sum = n+1 are (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). That's n points. Can a sunny line cover all these n points? The line through (1,n) and (n,1) has slope (1-n)/(n-1) = -1, which is not sunny. So no. So we cannot omit a diagonal and cover the remaining points with a single sunny line. So we need a different selection of non-sunny lines. For n=6, we used s=3,4,5,6,7 (five diagonals) and y=x (sunny) to cover all points. That's a construction: take all diagonals except the first two? Actually we omitted s=2 (which covers (1,1) only) and used y=x to cover (1,1). So that's a different pattern: we used two horizontal/vertical lines and two diagonals. So k=1 is possible for all n? We need to check if we can always construct a configuration with exactly one sunny line. For n=3, we used y=1,x=1,y=x. That's 2 non-sunny and 1 sunny. For n=4, we used x+y=5,y=1,x=1,y=x. That's 3 non-sunny and 1 sunny. For n=5, we used x+y=6,y=1,x=1,y=x+1? Actually we used y=1,x=1,x+y=5,x+y=6 and y=x+1. That's 4 non-sunny and 1 sunny. For n=6, we used s=3,4,5,6,7 and y=x. Then y=x covers (1,1) and also (2,2),(3,3),...,(n,n) but those points have sum >=4, so they are already covered. So all points are covered. The lines are distinct: y=x is sunny; the diagonals are non-sunny (since slope -1). So exactly 1 sunny line. So k=1 is possible for all n>=3. So k=1 is always possible.

Now, what about k=2? Can we always construct a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines? For n=3, we tried and failed. For n=4,5,6 we tried and failed. But maybe for larger n, it becomes possible. Let's analyze the general possibility.

We need n lines, exactly k sunny, covering all points. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. The sunny lines can cover some points as well. The total points are n(n+1)/2. The maximum number of points a sunny line can cover is floor((n+1)/2) (when slope 1 and shifted to maximize points). For n=7, that's 4. So two sunny lines could cover up to 8 points. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n-2)*n points if they are disjoint, but they will overlap. The best we can do with non-sunny lines is to use the diagonals: they cover all points. So if we use all diagonals, that's n lines, k=0. To have k=2, we need to replace 2 diagonals with 2 sunny lines, and still cover all points. That is, we need to choose 2 sunny lines such that the union of the remaining n-2 diagonals covers all points except possibly the points on the sunny lines, and the sunny lines themselves cover those points. So we need to find a set of 2 sunny lines that are not parallel to the diagonals (i.e., slopes not 0, undefined, -1) such that the points on those lines are exactly the points that would be missed by the diagonals we keep. Since we are keeping n-2 diagonals, we are omitting 2 diagonals. Which diagonals we omit? We can choose which diagonals to omit. The diagonals are x+y = s for s=2,...,n+1. If we omit two diagonals, say s = s1 and s = s2, then the points with those sums are not covered by the kept diagonals. So we need the two sunny lines to cover exactly those points (or at least those points, and possibly also cover some points that are already covered). So we need to find two sunny lines that together contain all points with sums s1 and s2. That is, the set of points with sums s1 and s2 must be a subset of the union of the two sunny lines. Since the points with a given sum s form a line of slope -1 (the diagonal itself). So the points with sum s1 are collinear on a line of slope -1. For a sunny line to contain all these points, it would have to be that line itself, but that line is not sunny. So no single sunny line can contain all points of a diagonal. However, two sunny lines could together contain the points of two diagonals. For example, if we take two sunny lines that are not parallel to the diagonals, they might intersect the diagonals in points. But we need to cover all points of the two diagonals. The points on a diagonal are many (s-1 points). For n large, each diagonal has many points. So it seems unlikely that two sunny lines can cover all points of two diagonals. But maybe we can choose the sunny lines to be lines that pass through many of those points. For instance, if we take two sunny lines that are both slope 1, they can cover points with constant difference. The points on a diagonal have constant sum, not constant difference. So a slope 1 line can contain at most one point from each diagonal? Actually consider a line y = x + c. The points on it satisfy a - b = -c. For a given sum s, the points (a,b) with a+b=s and a-b = -c => solving: a = (s - c)/2, b = (s + c)/2. So there is at most one point from each diagonal on a given slope 1 line. So a slope 1 line can contain at most one point from each diagonal. Therefore, to cover all points of two diagonals, we would need many slope 1 lines, not just two. So k=2 seems impossible if we rely on covering two entire diagonals with two sunny lines.

But maybe we can choose a different set of non-sunny lines that are not all diagonals. For instance, we could use horizontal and vertical lines to cover many points, and then use two sunny lines to cover the rest. The total points are n(n+1)/2. The maximum points a horizontal line can cover is n (when y=1). Similarly vertical. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, we could potentially cover many points. For example, use y=1 (covers n points), x=1 (covers n points), and then maybe some other lines. But they overlap at (1,1). So union size = 2n-1. For n large, that's much less than n(n+1)/2. So we would need many sunny lines to cover the rest. But we only have 2 sunny lines. So we need the non-sunny lines to cover most points. The best we can do with non-sunny lines is to use all diagonals, which covers all points. So to have k=2, we need to replace 2 diagonals with 2 sunny lines, and still cover all points. As argued, that seems impossible because the points on the omitted diagonals are many and cannot be covered by just 2 sunny lines.

But perhaps we can choose the non-sunny lines to be a mix that leaves a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Thus for n=5, k=2 seems impossible. So for n=7, k=2 also seems impossible.

Given the pattern, it might be that for all n>=3, the only possible k are 0 and 1. But we need to prove that k cannot be 2 or more. Let's try to analyze the general case.

We have n lines. Let the lines be L1,...,Ln. Among them, exactly k are sunny. The other n-k are non-sunny, i.e., they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b>=1, a+b <= n+1.

Observation: The points form a triangular grid. Consider the set of points with a+b = s for s=2,...,n+1. Each such diagonal is a line of slope -1. The non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover entire diagonals. The non-sunny lines that are horizontal or vertical cover points with constant b or a. The sunny lines have other slopes.

We need to show that if k >= 2, then it's impossible. Let's try to find an invariant or a counting argument.

One approach: Consider the number of points on each line. For a given line, the number of points from the triangle it contains is at most n (for non-sunny lines) and at most floor((n+1)/2) for sunny lines (since the maximum number of lattice points on a line with slope not 0, undefined, -1 that lie in the triangle is when the line is of slope 1 and passes through (1,1) and (n,n) but (n,n) has sum 2n > n+1 for n>=3, so the maximum is floor((n+1)/2)). Actually for n=5, floor(6/2)=3, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) -> 3 points. For n=7, floor(8/2)=4, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) -> 4 points. So a sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points.

Now, the total number of points is n(n+1)/2. If we have n lines, the sum of the number of points on each line (counting multiplicities) is at least n(n+1)/2, because each point must be covered by at least one line. But we can have overlaps. So we have:
∑_{i=1}^{n} |L_i ∩ T| ≥ n(n+1)/2, where T is the triangle.

Let’s denote for each line i, let p_i = |L_i ∩ T|. For non-sunny lines, p_i ≤ n. For sunny lines, p_i ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So we have:
∑_{i=1}^{n} p_i ≥ n(n+1)/2.

If we have k sunny lines, then the maximum possible sum of p_i is when the non-sunny lines are as efficient as possible (i.e., each covers n points) and the sunny lines are as efficient as possible (each covers floor((n+1)/2) points). So the maximum sum is (n-k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2). For n≥3, floor((n+1)/2) ≤ (n+1)/2. So the maximum sum is at most (n-k)*n + k*(n+1)/2 = n^2 - k n + (k n + k)/2 = n^2 - (k n)/2 + k/2 = n^2 - (k(n-1))/2. So the maximum sum is n^2 - k(n-1)/2. We need this maximum to be at least n(n+1)/2 = (n^2+n)/2. So we need:
n^2 - k(n-1)/2 ≥ (n^2+n)/2 => Multiply by 2: 2n^2 - k(n-1) ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - k(n-1) ≥ n => n^2 - n ≥ k(n-1) => n(n-1) ≥ k(n-1) => n ≥ k. So k ≤ n, which is always true. So the sum condition doesn't restrict k.

But we need to consider that the non-sunny lines might not be able to achieve the maximum simultaneously because they may overlap. However, the maximum sum is an upper bound, not a lower bound. We need the actual sum to be at least the total points. So we need to see if we can achieve the sum with the given lines. The sum of p_i can be larger than the total points due to overlaps, but we need it to be at least the total points. So the condition is that the maximum possible sum (which is an upper bound) must be at least the total points, which is true. So no contradiction.

We need a stronger argument. Perhaps consider the number of points on each diagonal. The diagonals are x+y = s for s=2,...,n+1. The non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover entire diagonals. The non-sunny horizontal or vertical lines cover points on multiple diagonals. The sunny lines cover at most one point per diagonal (since they are not parallel to the diagonals). So each sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal. Therefore, the total number of points on the diagonals that are covered by sunny lines is at most k times the number of diagonals? Actually each sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal, so for a given diagonal, the number of sunny lines that contain a point from that diagonal is at most k (since each sunny line can contain at most one point from that diagonal). But we need to cover all points on all diagonals. The points on a diagonal are s-1 points. The non-sunny lines can cover many points on a diagonal. In particular, a slope -1 line (non-sunny) can cover an entire diagonal. So the best way to cover the triangle is to use all diagonals. If we use some sunny lines, we must compensate by using non-sunny lines that cover the points that would be missed by the sunny lines.

Let's denote by D_s the set of points with sum s. |D_s| = s-1. The non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover entire D_s. The non-sunny lines that are horizontal or vertical can cover at most one point from each D_s? Actually a horizontal line y=c intersects D_s at points (a,c) with a+c=s => a=s-c. So there is at most one point from D_s on a given horizontal line. Similarly for vertical. So horizontal/vertical lines can contain at most one point per diagonal. Sunny lines also contain at most one point per diagonal. So each line that is not a slope -1 diagonal can contain at most one point from each D_s. Therefore, the total number of points from D_s that can be covered by lines that are not slope -1 diagonals is at most (number of such lines) * 1. So if we have many sunny lines, we need many slope -1 diagonals to cover the rest of the points on each diagonal.

Now, consider the diagonals. The total points on all diagonals is n(n+1)/2. Suppose we have k sunny lines. They can cover at most k points from each diagonal (since each sunny line can cover at most one point from each diagonal). So the total points covered by sunny lines across all diagonals is at most k * (number of diagonals) = k * n. But that's not a strong bound because k*n could be less than total points.

But we need to cover all points. The non-sunny lines can cover points on diagonals. If we use a slope -1 diagonal line, it can cover an entire diagonal. So we can cover many points with few lines. The key is that the sunny lines cannot cover more than one point per diagonal, so they are inefficient for covering the many points on a diagonal. Therefore, to cover the triangle, we need many non-sunny lines that are slope -1 to cover the diagonals. The total number of non-sunny lines is n-k. If we use all of them as slope -1 diagonals, they can cover at most n-k diagonals. That would leave k diagonals uncovered. Those k diagonals have total points = sum_{s in uncovered} (s-1). For the triangle to be covered, the sunny lines must cover all points on those k diagonals. But each sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal, so to cover all points on a diagonal, we would need many sunny lines. Specifically, if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points from a given diagonal. So if a diagonal has more than k points, it cannot be covered entirely by sunny lines. Therefore, the diagonals we omit must have at most k points each. That means the omitted diagonals must be among the smallest ones: s=2,...,k+1? Because the size of D_s is s-1. So we need s-1 ≤ k => s ≤ k+1. So the omitted diagonals can only be those with s ≤ k+1. So the number of such diagonals is at most k. But we are omitting n-k diagonals? Wait, we are using n-k non-sunny lines. If we use them all as slope -1 diagonals, they cover n-k diagonals. That leaves k diagonals uncovered (since there are n+1 diagonals? Actually there are n diagonals? For n lines, the triangle has points with sums from 2 to n+1, that's n diagonals. Yes, because sum=2,...,n+1 gives n diagonals. So there are n diagonals. If we use n-k slope -1 lines, they cover n-k diagonals, leaving k diagonals uncovered. So the uncovered diagonals are exactly k in number. For the sunny lines to cover all points on those k diagonals, each such diagonal must have at most k points (since each sunny line can cover at most one point from each diagonal, and there are k sunny lines, so the maximum points from a diagonal that can be covered by sunny lines is k). Therefore, the uncovered diagonals must be those with size ≤ k, i.e., s-1 ≤ k => s ≤ k+1. So the uncovered diagonals must be among the first k+1 diagonals? Actually the diagonals are indexed by s=2,...,n+1. The condition s ≤ k+1 means s=2,...,k+1. That's k diagonals. So the uncovered diagonals must be exactly the first k diagonals? There are k diagonals with s=2,...,k+1. So the uncovered diagonals are exactly those. That means the n-k slope -1 diagonals we keep must be the ones with s ≥ k+2? Because we need to cover all points on the diagonals we keep, and the sunny lines cover the points on the first k diagonals. So the kept diagonals are s = k+2, k+3, ..., n+1. That's (n+1) - (k+1) = n-k diagonals, which matches the number of non-sunny lines. So this is a plausible covering: use the diagonals s = k+2, ..., n+1 as non-sunny lines, and use k sunny lines to cover the points on the first k diagonals (s=2,...,k+1). So the problem reduces to: For a given n and k (with 0 ≤ k ≤ n), can we find k sunny lines such that their union contains all points of the first k diagonals? The first k diagonals are D_2, D_3, ..., D_{k+1}. Their total points are sum_{s=2}^{k+1} (s-1) = sum_{t=1}^{k} t = k(k+1)/2. So we need to cover these k(k+1)/2 points with k sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points, but more importantly, each sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal (since it's not parallel to the diagonals). So across the k diagonals, each sunny line can contain at most k points (one from each diagonal). But we need to cover all points on these diagonals. The total points on these diagonals is k(k+1)/2. With k lines, each covering at most k points, the maximum total points covered (counting multiplicities) is k*k = k^2. But we need to cover k(k+1)/2 points. For k≥2, k(k+1)/2 > k^2? Actually k(k+1)/2 ≤ k^2? For k=2, 3 ≤4; for k=3, 6 ≤9; for k=4, 10 ≤16; so it's possible in terms of size. However, we need the actual points to be covered. The points on a diagonal are collinear on a line of slope -1. A sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal. So the coverage of the diagonals by sunny lines is like a set of k lines (sunny) that together contain all points of the first k diagonals. That means for each diagonal D_s (s=2,...,k+1), the points of D_s must be a subset of the union of the k sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most one point from D_s, the union of the k sunny lines can contain at most k points from D_s. But D_s has s-1 points. For s=2, D_2 has 1 point; for s=3, D_3 has 2 points; for s=4, D_4 has 3 points; etc. So for s > k+1? Actually for s up to k+1, the size of D_s is s-1. For s=2, size1; s=3, size2; ... s=k+1, size k. So the largest diagonal among the first k diagonals is D_{k+1} with size k. So each such diagonal has at most k points. Therefore, it is possible that k sunny lines cover all points of D_{k+1} if each sunny line takes exactly one point from D_{k+1}. Similarly, for D_{k}, size k-1, we need k sunny lines to cover all its points, which is possible if each sunny line takes at most one point from D_k, and we have k lines, so we can cover all k-1 points. So the condition is that we need to assign to each sunny line a point from each diagonal such that the union of the points on each diagonal is exactly the entire diagonal. Since each diagonal has at most k points, we need to cover all its points with the k sunny lines. That is equivalent to saying that the k sunny lines, when restricted to the first k diagonals, form a set of k lines that together contain all points of those diagonals. This is a combinatorial design problem: we need to find k lines (with slopes not 0, undefined, -1) such that for each s=2,...,k+1, the set of points (a,b) with a+b=s is a subset of the union of these lines, and each line can contain at most one point from each such diagonal. Since the lines are distinct and not parallel to the diagonals, they can intersect each diagonal in at most one point. So we need to choose k points on each diagonal (one per sunny line) such that the k points on each diagonal are exactly the points of that diagonal. That means we need to select a set of k points (one per sunny line) from each diagonal, and the union of the k sunny lines must contain all points of the diagonal. Since the diagonal has exactly k points (for s=k+1), we need each sunny line to take a distinct point from D_{k+1}. Similarly, for D_{k}, we need the k sunny lines to cover its k-1 points, so one sunny line will not cover a point from D_k? Actually we need to cover all points of D_k. So the main difficulty is to find k lines (sunny) that pass through the points of D_{k+1} and also through the points of the other diagonals, with the condition that each line contains exactly one point from each diagonal (since it can contain at most one, but it may contain none from some diagonals? So the sunny lines must pass through the points of D_{k+1} and also through the points of the other diagonals, with the condition that each sunny line contains exactly one point from D_{k+1} and at most one from each other diagonal.

Thus the problem reduces to: For a given n and k (with 0 ≤ k ≤ n), can we find k lines (sunny) such that they pass through all points of D_{k+1} (i.e., the diagonal with sum k+1) and also through all points of the other diagonals D_2,...,D_k? Actually we need to cover all points of D_2,...,D_{k+1}. The points of D_{k+1} are (1,k),(2,k-1),...,(k,1). So we need k lines that together contain these k points. Since each line can contain at most one point from D_{k+1}, we need each line to contain exactly one of these points. So the sunny lines must be such that they each pass through one of the points of D_{k+1}. Moreover, they must also pass through the points of the other diagonals. For D_s (s=2,...,k), the points are (1,s-1),(2,s-2),...,(s-1,1). So we need that for each such diagonal, all its points lie on the union of the k sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most one point from D_s, we need that the number of sunny lines that contain a point from D_s is at least |D_s| = s-1. That's possible because we have k lines, and s-1 ≤ k for s ≤ k+1. So we need to assign to each point of D_s a sunny line that contains it. This is like a covering of the triangular grid of side k by k lines. This is reminiscent of the fact that the only way to cover all points of the triangle with k lines is to use the diagonals themselves. But here the lines are not parallel to the diagonals; they have other slopes. So we need to see if there exists a set of k lines (with slopes not 0, undefined, -1) such that they contain all points of the triangle of side k (i.e., the points with a,b>=1, a+b ≤ k+1). That is exactly the problem for n=k. We already know that for n=k, the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points is k (using the diagonals). But can we cover with k lines that are all sunny? Thus for n odd, k=n is impossible because the maximum points a sunny line can cover is floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2, and with n lines, the total points covered (counting overlaps) is at most n*(n+1)/2 = n(n+1)/2, which equals the total points. So it's possible in terms of size, but the collinearity constraints (each line can contain at most one point from each diagonal) force that the points on the largest diagonal must be covered by distinct lines, and those lines must also cover the other points. However, as we saw for n=5, it's impossible. In general, for n odd, the triangle of side n has points with sums 2,...,n+1. The largest diagonal has size n. So each sunny line must contain exactly one point from that diagonal. So the sunny lines are determined by the points on that diagonal. Moreover, each sunny line can contain at most one point from each other diagonal. So to cover all points, the sunny lines must together contain all points of each diagonal. This is equivalent to covering the triangle of side n with n lines that each pass through a distinct point of the diagonal with sum n+1. This is a known problem: the only way to cover the triangle with n lines is to use the diagonals themselves. But here the lines are not allowed to be parallel to the diagonals. So it's impossible. More formally, we can prove by induction or by counting that if k >= 2, then the configuration cannot cover all points.

Let's try to prove that for any n >= 3, the only possible k are 0 and 1.

We already have constructions for k=0 and k=1. So we need to show that k cannot be >= 2.

Assume we have n lines with exactly k sunny lines, covering all points. Let the non-sunny lines be N = n-k. They are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The sunny lines are S = k.

Consider the set of points with sum = n+1 (the largest diagonal). There are n points. Each non-sunny line that is slope -1 can cover an entire diagonal, so if any such line exists, it could cover the entire diagonal. However, we have only n-k non-sunny lines. If we use a slope -1 line, it covers the entire diagonal. So to cover the n points on that diagonal, we could have one slope -1 line covering all of them. That would be efficient. But we need to cover all points, not just that diagonal. The other diagonals also need to be covered. The sunny lines can cover at most one point per diagonal. So if we have many sunny lines, we need many non-sunny lines to cover the points on the diagonals. The key is that the total number of points on the triangle is n(n+1)/2. The sum of the number of points on each line (counting multiplicities) is at least n(n+1)/2. For each sunny line, the number of points it contains is at most floor((n+1)/2). For each non-sunny line, it is at most n. So we have:
∑_{i=1}^{n} p_i ≥ n(n+1)/2.
Let’s denote by p_i the number of triangle points on line i. For non-sunny lines, p_i ≤ n. For sunny lines, p_i ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So the maximum possible sum is (n-k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2). For k ≥ 2, we need to see if this maximum can be at least n(n+1)/2. As we computed, this gives n ≥ k, which is always true. So no contradiction.

But we can consider a stronger bound: For each diagonal D_s, the number of points on it that can be covered by sunny lines is at most k (since each sunny line can contain at most one point from D_s). So the total number of points on D_s that are covered by sunny lines is ≤ k. The remaining points on D_s must be covered by non-sunny lines. Non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover entire D_s if they are that diagonal. But non-sunny lines that are horizontal or vertical can cover at most one point from D_s. So if we have many sunny lines, we need many non-sunny lines that are slope -1 to cover the points on the diagonals. But we only have n-k non-sunny lines. So we need to see if we can cover all points on the diagonals with n-k non-sunny lines and k sunny lines.

Let's denote by a_s the number of points on D_s that are covered by sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most one point from D_s, a_s ≤ k. The points on D_s that are not covered by sunny lines must be covered by non-sunny lines. Non-sunny lines can be of three types. If a non-sunny line is slope -1, it can cover all points of D_s if it is exactly D_s. If it is horizontal or vertical, it can cover at most one point from D_s. So to cover the s-1 points on D_s, we need either one slope -1 line (which covers all points) or many horizontal/vertical lines (each covering at most one). Since we have only n-k non-sunny lines, and we need to cover all diagonals, we need to allocate the non-sunny lines to cover the diagonals. The most efficient way is to use slope -1 lines to cover entire diagonals. So we should use as many slope -1 lines as possible. Let’s denote by m the number of non-sunny lines that are slope -1. Then the remaining n-k-m non-sunny lines are horizontal or vertical. They can cover at most one point per diagonal each. So the total points from the diagonals that can be covered by non-sunny lines (including slope -1) is: each slope -1 line covers an entire diagonal (size s-1). The horizontal/vertical lines cover at most one point per diagonal. So the total coverage of the diagonals by non-sunny lines is at most sum of sizes of the diagonals they cover (if they are slope -1) plus (n-k-m) points (since each horizontal/vertical line can cover at most one point from each diagonal, but we need to consider that they can cover points from multiple diagonals; actually a horizontal line y=c covers points on multiple diagonals: for each a such that a+c ≤ n+1, it covers a point on D_{a+c}. So it covers one point from each diagonal it intersects. So the total number of points from diagonals that can be covered by horizontal/vertical lines is at most (n-k-m) * (number of diagonals they intersect). But that's not a simple bound.

Better approach: Use a double counting argument on the number of points on each line and the number of points on each diagonal. Since the sunny lines are not parallel to the diagonals, they intersect each diagonal in at most one point. So the total number of points on the triangle that lie on sunny lines is at most k * n (since there are n diagonals, each sunny line contributes at most one point per diagonal, but the total points on sunny lines is the sum over sunny lines of the number of triangle points they contain, which is at most k * floor((n+1)/2). That's not a bound on the number of points on diagonals.

Maybe we can use an invariant: Consider the sum of the coordinates of the points. For each point (a,b), the sum a+b is between 2 and n+1. The lines have equations. For a line that is not slope -1, the number of points from the triangle it contains is at most floor((n+1)/2). For a line that is slope -1, it can contain many points. The key is that the sunny lines cannot contain more than one point from each diagonal. So if we have many sunny lines, the total number of points from the triangle that are on sunny lines is at most k * floor((n+1)/2). But we need to cover all points. So the points on sunny lines are at most k * floor((n+1)/2). The remaining points must be on non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n-k)*n points in total (since each non-sunny line can cover at most n points). So the total points covered by all lines is at most k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n. For this to be at least n(n+1)/2, we need:
k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2.
For n odd, floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2. Then inequality becomes:
k*(n+1)/2 + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => Multiply by 2: k(n+1) + 2n(n-k) ≥ n(n+1) => k(n+1) + 2n^2 - 2nk ≥ n^2 + n => 2n^2 - 2nk + k(n+1) ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - 2nk + k(n+1) ≥ n => n^2 - 2nk + kn + k ≥ n => n^2 - nk + k ≥ n => n^2 - nk + k - n ≥ 0 => n(n-1) - k(n-1) ≥ 0 => (n-1)(n-k) ≥ 0, which is true. So no contradiction.

For n even, floor((n+1)/2) = n/2. Then inequality: k*(n/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => Multiply by 2: k n + 2n(n-k) ≥ n(n+1) => k n + 2n^2 - 2nk ≥ n^2 + n => 2n^2 - 2nk + kn ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - nk ≥ n => n^2 - nk - n ≥ 0 => n(n - k - 1) ≥ 0 => n ≥ k+1 => k ≤ n-1. So for n even, k cannot be n. So k ≤ n-1. So the necessary condition from maximum coverage is that k ≤ n-1 for even n. So k=n is impossible for even n. For odd n, k could be n in principle. But we already argued that k=n is impossible due to collinearity constraints. So we need to show that for any n≥3, k cannot be ≥2.

Let's try to prove that if k ≥ 2, then the configuration cannot cover all points. Perhaps we can use a parity or modulo argument.

Another idea: Consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (the largest diagonal). There are n points. Each sunny line can contain at most one of these points. So the number of sunny lines that contain a point from this diagonal is at most k. But we need to cover all n points. So the non-sunny lines must cover the remaining n - (number of sunny lines that intersect this diagonal) points. Non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover the entire diagonal. If we use a slope -1 line that is exactly the diagonal x+y = n+1, it covers all n points. So we could have one such line covering all points. That would be efficient. But we need to cover the other points as well. So it's plausible to have a set of non-sunny lines that include that diagonal. Then the sunny lines can cover other points. So the problem is to see if we can have k sunny lines covering the rest.

Suppose we have a configuration with k sunny lines. Consider the set of points on the diagonal D_{n+1}. Let t be the number of sunny lines that contain a point from D_{n+1}. Since each sunny line can contain at most one point from D_{n+1}, t ≤ k. The remaining n-t points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Non-sunny lines that are slope -1 can cover entire diagonals. If we have a non-sunny line that is exactly D_{n+1}, it covers all n points. So if we have such a line, then t can be 0. But we also need to cover the other points. So it's possible to have a non-sunny line covering D_{n+1}. So we can assume without loss of generality that we have a slope -1 line covering D_{n+1}. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover entire diagonals. So we could use the diagonals D_2,...,D_n as non-sunny lines, giving n-1 lines covering all points except D_{n+1}? Actually D_2 has 1 point, D_3 has 2, ..., D_n has n-1 points. The total points on D_2 through D_n is 1+2+...+(n-1)=n(n-1)/2. So if we take all those diagonals as non-sunny lines, they cover all points except D_{n+1}. And we already have a non-sunny line covering D_{n+1}. So total non-sunny lines = n, all slope -1. That gives k=0. So to have k>0, we must replace some of these diagonals with sunny lines. So we need to see if we can replace some diagonals with sunny lines while still covering all points. That is, we need to find a set of k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines (which could be a mix of diagonals and horizontal/vertical) such that the union covers all points. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to be diagonals. So suppose we keep the diagonals D_2,...,D_{n-k+1} as non-sunny lines? Actually we have n-k non-sunny lines. If we use them as diagonals, they can cover at most n-k diagonals. The points on the remaining k diagonals (the largest ones) must be covered by the sunny lines. So the uncovered diagonals are those with sums s = n-k+2, ..., n+1? Wait, we have n diagonals total (sums 2 to n+1). If we keep n-k diagonals, we can choose which ones. The most efficient is to keep the smallest ones because they have fewer points? Actually we want to minimize the points that need to be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most k points per diagonal (since each sunny line can cover at most one point from a diagonal). So if we leave out k diagonals, the total points on those diagonals is sum_{s in omitted} (s-1). To have these points covered by k sunny lines, we need that each such diagonal has at most k points. That means the omitted diagonals must be among those with size ≤ k. So the omitted diagonals must be the largest ones? Actually the size of D_s is s-1. For s ≤ k+1, size ≤ k. So the omitted diagonals can be at most the first k diagonals? Wait, we have n diagonals. If we omit k diagonals, we want the omitted diagonals to have size ≤ k. The largest diagonals have size n, n-1, etc., which are > k for k small. So we cannot omit large diagonals because they have many points and cannot be covered by k sunny lines. Therefore, for n≥3, the only possible k are those for which we can cover the first m diagonals with m sunny lines, where m = n - (number of non-sunny lines used to cover the largest diagonals). Since we have n lines total, and we can choose any set of non-sunny lines, we can think of covering the triangle with n lines, where some lines are sunny and some are non-sunny. The non-sunny lines can be diagonals covering the largest diagonals. The sunny lines must cover the remaining diagonals. So if we let m be the number of sunny lines, then we need to cover the first m diagonals (the smallest ones) with m sunny lines. But we also have n-m non-sunny lines covering the largest n-m diagonals. However, we could also use non-sunny lines that are not diagonals, but that would be less efficient. So the best chance to have many sunny lines is to use the non-sunny lines as diagonals covering the largest diagonals. So the possible k are those for which the triangle of side k (the first k diagonals) can be covered by k sunny lines. As we saw, for k=1, yes; k=2, no; k=3, yes; k=4, no; k=5, no; k=6, no; k=7, no; etc. So the only possible k are 0,1,3? But we also found k=4 works for n=6. So we used more sunny lines than the number of diagonals. So the condition is not that we need to cover the first k diagonals with k sunny lines; we can have more sunny lines than the number of diagonals. In fact, for n=6, we used 4 sunny lines to cover 6 points. That's possible because the sunny lines can cover 2 points each. So the general strategy: We have n lines. We can choose some non-sunny lines to cover the largest diagonals. The remaining points (on the smallest diagonals) must be covered by the sunny lines. The number of sunny lines is k. They can cover at most k * floor((n+1)/2) points in total, but they also have the constraint that each sunny line can contain at most one point from each diagonal. So to cover the points on the first t diagonals (sums 2 to t+1), we need k sunny lines such that the union of these lines contains all those points. The points on these diagonals are the triangle of side t. The number of points is t(t+1)/2. We need to cover these points with k lines. Each line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points, but more importantly, each line can contain at most one point from each diagonal. So the maximum number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines is at most k * t (since there are t diagonals, each sunny line can contribute at most 1 point per diagonal, but the lines may not intersect all diagonals; they can contribute at most 1 per diagonal, so total points from the triangle is at most k * t). However, we need to cover t(t+1)/2 points. So we need k * t ≥ t(t+1)/2 => k ≥ (t+1)/2. So the number of sunny lines must be at least about t/2. That's a necessary condition. Also, we need that the actual points can be covered. This is like a covering of the triangular grid of side t by k lines that are not parallel to the diagonals. This is a known combinatorial geometry problem: The only way to cover the triangle with lines that are not parallel to the diagonals is to use lines of slope 1 (or other slopes) that cover at most 2 points per line for t=3, etc. We can try to find all possible k for given n.

We have constructions:
- For n=3: k=0,1,3
- For n=4: k=0,1
- For n=5: k=0,1,3
- For n=6: k=0,1,4
- For n=7: we can try to find k=5? Let's attempt to construct for n=7 with k=5. We need 5 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be diagonals covering the largest diagonals. The points on the remaining diagonals (the smallest ones) must be covered by the 5 sunny lines. The number of diagonals is 7. If we use 2 non-sunny diagonals, we have 5 remaining diagonals. The points on those 5 diagonals are 1+2+3+4+5=15 points. So we need to cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most 1 point from each diagonal, so the maximum total points from the triangle is 5*5=25, but we need to cover 15 points. So it's possible in terms of size. However, we need to see if we can actually cover the triangle of side 5 (15 points) with 5 sunny lines. As we argued, the only sunny lines that can contain more than 2 points are those with slope 1, and only y=x gives 3 points. So we cannot get 5 lines each covering 3 points. But we could have some lines covering 2 points and some covering 3 points. For example, we could use y=x (covers 3 points: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)), y=x+1 (covers 2 points: (2,1),(3,2) but (3,2) is in triangle? For n=7, triangle points have sum ≤8, so (3,2) sum5, yes; (4,3) sum7, (5,4) sum9 not, so y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2) only 2 points), y=x-1 (covers (1,2),(2,3) only 2 points), y=x+2 (covers (3,1),(4,2) only 2 points), y=x-2 (covers (1,3),(2,4) only 2 points), etc. So we could have a set of 5 sunny lines covering the 15 points. For instance, use y=x (3 points), y=x+1 (2 points), y=x-1 (2 points), y=x+2 (2 points), y=x-2 (2 points) gives total 3+2+2+2+2=11 points, but we need 15 points. So not enough. We could also use lines that are not slope 1 but still cover 2 points. For example, line through (1,5) and (2,4) has slope -1, not sunny. So no. So the maximum points from 5 sunny lines is at most 5*3=15 if they are all slope 1 lines with 3 points each. But we only have one such line (y=x) that gives 3 points. The other slope 1 lines give at most 2 points. So the maximum total points from 5 sunny lines is 3 + 2*4 = 11, which is less than 15. So we cannot cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines. Thus k=5 fails for n=7.

What about k=4 for n=7? Need 4 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover the largest diagonals. The points on the remaining diagonals (the smallest ones) must be covered by the 4 sunny lines. If we use 3 non-sunny diagonals, we have 4 remaining diagonals. The maximum points a sunny line can cover in the triangle of side 4 is floor((4+1)/2)=2. So 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points (if each covers 2 points). But we need 10 points. So impossible. Need 4 sunny lines to cover 10 points, max 8. So no. For n=9, we can try to find possible k. The pattern seems that for n=3, k=3 works; n=5, k=3 works; n=7, k=3 and 4 work; n=9, maybe k=5 works? Let's analyze the general problem.

We have n lines. We want to cover the triangle of side n. The points are on diagonals D_s for s=2,...,n+1. The non-sunny lines can be chosen as diagonals covering the largest t diagonals. The number of sunny lines is k = n - t (since total lines n, non-sunny t). So we need to cover the triangle of side m = n-t with m sunny lines. So the problem reduces to: For a given m (where m = n - t, and t is the number of non-sunny lines used, which we can choose), can we cover the triangle of side m with m sunny lines? If yes, then we have a configuration with k = m sunny lines and t = n-m non-sunny lines. So the possible k are those m for which the triangle of side m can be covered by m sunny lines. We have seen that for m=1, yes; m=2, no; m=3, yes; m=4, no; m=5, no; m=6, no; m=7, no; m=8, no; etc. So only m=1 and m=3 are possible? Because we used non-sunny lines covering sums 5 and 6, leaving sums 2-4 (6 points). So m = n - t = 6-2=4, but the remaining triangle is not of side 4; it's of side 3? That line contains (6,1) and (2,2) (which is in sum4). So it could cover (6,1) and (2,2). Similarly, line through (5,2) and (2,3) slope (3-2)/(2-5)=1/(-3)=-1/3, sunny. That line contains (5,2) and (2,3) (sum5). So we could try to cover the sum7 points with the sunny lines we already have. But we have 4 sunny lines, and we need to cover 6 sum7 points. So k=4 for n=6 is impossible.

Thus we need to find the correct possible k for each n.

Let's systematically analyze the covering problem.

We have n lines. Let’s denote the set of points T = {(a,b) ∈ ℤ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. Total points = n(n+1)/2.

We need to choose n distinct lines such that every point in T lies on at least one line. Among these lines, exactly k are sunny (not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). The other n-k lines are non-sunny, i.e., they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

We want to determine all possible k for which such a configuration exists.

Observations:
- The points lie on n diagonals (x+y = s for s=2,...,n+1). Each diagonal has s-1 points.
- A non-sunny line that is slope -1 can cover an entire diagonal. A horizontal or vertical line can cover at most one point from each diagonal.
- A sunny line can cover at most one point from each diagonal (since it is not parallel to the diagonals). So each sunny line can intersect each diagonal in at most one point.

Let’s denote by d_s the set of points on diagonal s. |d_s| = s-1.

We have n lines total. Let’s denote by L_i the lines. For each line i, let p_i = |L_i ∩ T|. For non-sunny lines, p_i can be up to n (if slope -1 and covering a diagonal). For sunny lines, p_i ≤ floor((n+1)/2) (since the maximum number of lattice points on a line with slope not 0, undefined, -1 that lie in T is achieved by slope 1 line through (1,1) and (n,n) but (n,n) not in T for n≥3, so the maximum is floor((n+1)/2)). Actually for n=3, floor(4/2)=2, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2) -> 2 points; y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2) but (3,2) sum5 not in T? For n=3, T has sum≤4, so (3,2) sum5 not; so only (2,1) ->1 point. So max is 2. For n=4, floor(5/2)=2, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum6 not in T? T sum≤5, so (3,3) sum6 not; so only (1,1),(2,2) ->2 points; y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2) but (3,2) sum5, yes; so 2 points; y=x-1 covers (1,2),(2,3) but (2,3) sum5, yes; so 2 points. So max is 2. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) ->3 points; y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (4,3) sum7 not in T? T sum≤6, so (4,3) sum7 not; so only 2 points; y=x-1 covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) but (3,4) sum7 not; so 2 points. So max is 3. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) ->3 points; y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) but (4,3) sum7, T sum≤7? For n=6, T sum≤7, so (4,3) sum7 is in T; so y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3) ->3 points; y=x-1 covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) ->3 points; y=x+2 covers (3,1),(4,2),(5,3) but (5,3) sum8 not; so 2 points; etc. So max is 3. For n=7, floor(8/2)=4, and y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) ->4 points; y=x+1 covers (2,1),(3,2),(4,3),(5,4) ->4 points; etc. So max is 4. So in general, the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain is floor((n+1)/2). And this maximum is achieved by lines of slope 1 that are shifted appropriately.

Now, consider the covering. Let’s denote by S the set of sunny lines, |S| = k. Let’s denote by N the set of non-sunny lines, |N| = n-k.

We can think of the lines as covering the points. Since the points are on diagonals, we can consider the number of points from each diagonal that are covered by sunny lines. Let a_s = |d_s ∩ (∪_{L∈S} L)|. Since each sunny line can contain at most one point from d_s, we have a_s ≤ k. The points on d_s that are not covered by sunny lines must be covered by non-sunny lines. Non-sunny lines can be of three types. If a non-sunny line is slope -1, it can cover the entire diagonal if it is exactly that diagonal. If it is horizontal or vertical, it can cover at most one point from d_s. So for each s, the number of points on d_s that are covered by non-sunny lines is at most (number of slope -1 lines that are exactly d_s) + (number of horizontal/vertical lines). But we have a limited number of non-sunny lines.

A key observation: The points on the largest diagonal d_{n+1} are n points. To cover these n points, we could use one slope -1 line that is exactly d_{n+1}. That would cover all n points with one non-sunny line. Alternatively, we could use many horizontal/vertical lines, each covering at most one point from d_{n+1}. Since we have only n-k non-sunny lines, if we use a slope -1 line to cover d_{n+1}, we use 1 line and cover all n points. If we use horizontal/vertical lines, we would need n lines to cover all n points, but we only have n-k lines, so we cannot cover all n points with horizontal/vertical lines unless k=0. So for k>0, we must use a slope -1 line to cover d_{n+1}. Similarly, for other diagonals, we can use slope -1 lines to cover them efficiently. So the optimal strategy is to use non-sunny lines as diagonals covering the largest diagonals. Since we have n-k non-sunny lines, we can cover the n-k largest diagonals (those with sums from n-k+2 to n+1) with one line each. Then the remaining points are those on the first n-k diagonals (sums 2 to n-k+1). These points must be covered by the k sunny lines. So we need to cover the triangle of side m = n-k (points with sums 2 to m+1) with k sunny lines. Note that m = n-k, and the number of sunny lines is k. So we need to cover the triangle of side m with m sunny lines? Wait, the number of points on the first m diagonals is m(m+1)/2. The number of sunny lines is k = n-m. So we need to cover m(m+1)/2 points with n-m sunny lines. That's not necessarily m sunny lines. So the condition is: For a given n, we can choose t = number of non-sunny lines used as diagonals covering the largest t diagonals. Then the remaining points are on the first n-t diagonals, which is the triangle of side n-t. The number of sunny lines is k = n-t. So we need to cover the triangle of side m = n-t with m sunny lines? Actually the number of sunny lines is k = n-t, and the triangle has side m = n-t. So we need to cover the triangle of side m with m sunny lines. So the possible k are those m for which the triangle of side m can be covered by m sunny lines. So we need to determine all m such that the triangular grid of side m (points with a,b>=1, a+b ≤ m+1) can be covered by m lines that are sunny (i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). We have seen that for m=1, yes (one line through the single point). For m=2, no (three points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines). Thus the only possible k are those m such that we can cover the triangle of side m with m sunny lines, where m = n - t, and t is the number of non-sunny lines used. But we are not forced to use all non-sunny lines as diagonals; we could use some horizontal/vertical lines instead. However, using horizontal/vertical lines is less efficient, so they would require more sunny lines to cover the remaining points. So the best chance to have many sunny lines is to use the non-sunny lines as diagonals covering the largest diagonals. If we use some horizontal/vertical lines, we would have fewer non-sunny lines covering the largest diagonals, leaving more points to be covered by sunny lines, which would require more sunny lines. Since the number of sunny lines is k, and the total lines is n, we have k = n - t. If we use t non-sunny lines, we can cover at most t diagonals. The remaining points are on the first n-t diagonals. The number of points on these diagonals is (n-t)(n-t+1)/2. These points must be covered by the k sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most one point from each diagonal, so the total number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines is at most k * (n-t) (since there are n-t diagonals). Therefore, we need k * (n-t) ≥ (n-t)(n-t+1)/2 => k ≥ (n-t+1)/2. Since k = n-t, this gives n-t ≥ (n-t+1)/2 => 2(n-t) ≥ n-t+1 => n-t ≥ 1, which is true. So the size condition is not restrictive. However, we also need that the actual points can be covered. The maximum points a sunny line can cover in the triangle of side n-t is floor((n-t+1)/2). So the total points that can be covered by k sunny lines is at most k * floor((n-t+1)/2). But we need to cover (n-t)(n-t+1)/2 points. So we need k * floor((n-t+1)/2) ≥ (n-t)(n-t+1)/2. Since k = n-t, this gives (n-t) * floor((n-t+1)/2) ≥ (n-t)(n-t+1)/2 => floor((n-t+1)/2) ≥ (n-t+1)/2 => which holds only if n-t is odd. So for n-t odd, the inequality is equality if floor((n-t+1)/2) = (n-t+1)/2, which is true when n-t is odd. For n-t even, floor((n-t+1)/2) = (n-t)/2, so the inequality becomes (n-t)*(n-t)/2 ≥ (n-t)(n-t+1)/2 => (n-t)^2 ≥ (n-t)(n-t+1) => n-t ≥ n-t+1 => 0 ≥ 1, false. So if n-t is even, then the maximum total points from k sunny lines is (n-t)*(n-t)/2 = (n-t)^2/2, but the number of points to cover is (n-t)(n-t+1)/2 = (n-t)^2/2 + (n-t)/2, which is larger. So it's impossible to cover the triangle of side m = n-t with m sunny lines if m is even. So m must be odd. For m odd, the inequality is equality, so it's possible in terms of size. But we also need that the actual points can be covered. For m=3, we have a construction. We need to find a general characterization.

Let's denote by m = n - k the number of non-sunny lines. We can use the non-sunny lines as diagonals covering the largest m diagonals. Then the remaining points are those on the first n-m diagonals, which is the triangle of side n-m. The number of sunny lines is k = n - m. So we need to cover the triangle of side n-m with n-m sunny lines. Let’s set t = n - m, so t = k. Then we need to cover the triangle of side t with t sunny lines. As we have seen, this is possible if and only if t = 0, 1, or 3? But we also have t=4? For n=10, we had k=4 works, which corresponds to t=4. But we just argued that covering the triangle of side 4 with 4 sunny lines is impossible because the maximum points per sunny line is 2, and 4*2=8 < 10. However, in our construction for n=10, we used non-sunny lines covering sums 5-8 (m=4) and sunny lines covering sums 2-4 (t=4? Wait, n=10, k=4 means 4 sunny lines and 6 non-sunny lines. So our t is not n-m; it's the side of the triangle that sunny lines need to cover. In the strategy where we use non-sunny lines as diagonals covering the largest m diagonals, the remaining triangle has side n-m = k. So we need to cover triangle of side k with k sunny lines. For n=10, k=4, we need to cover triangle of side 4 with 4 sunny lines. That is impossible. But we used a different selection: we used non-sunny lines covering sums 5-8 (which are not the largest 6 diagonals; they are sums 5,6,7,8, which are the 4 largest among the first 8? Actually we have 9 diagonals (sums 2-10). The largest 6 are sums 5-10. But we used sums 5-8, which are 4 lines. That's not covering the largest m diagonals; it's covering a different set. So the optimal strategy is not necessarily to use the largest diagonals as non-sunny lines. We can use some horizontal/vertical lines to cover more points, leaving a triangle of side t that can be covered by t sunny lines. So the problem is more general.

We need to find all k for which there exists a covering of the triangle of side n with n lines, exactly k of which are sunny. This is a combinatorial covering problem. We can try to think of the lines as covering the points. Since the points are in a triangular grid, we can consider the number of points on each line.

One approach: Use the concept of "sunny lines" as lines that are not parallel to the axes or the diagonal x+y=0. The maximum number of points a sunny line can contain is floor((n+1)/2). So with k sunny lines, the maximum total points they can cover (counting overlaps) is k * floor((n+1)/2). The non-sunny lines can cover at most (n-k)*n points (if each covers n points). So the total union is at most k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n. But we need this to be at least n(n+1)/2. This gives a necessary condition: k*floor((n+1)/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2. For n even, floor((n+1)/2) = n/2. Then condition: k*(n/2) + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => (kn/2) + n^2 - kn ≥ (n^2+n)/2 => n^2 - (kn/2) ≥ (n^2+n)/2 => Multiply by 2: 2n^2 - kn ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - n ≥ kn => n(n-1) ≥ kn => n-1 ≥ k => k ≤ n-1. So for n even, k cannot be n. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd, floor((n+1)/2) = (n+1)/2. Then condition: k*(n+1)/2 + (n-k)*n ≥ n(n+1)/2 => Multiply by 2: k(n+1) + 2n(n-k) ≥ n(n+1) => kn + k + 2n^2 - 2kn ≥ n^2 + n => 2n^2 - kn + k ≥ n^2 + n => n^2 - n ≥ kn - k => n(n-1) ≥ k(n-1) => n ≥ k. So k ≤ n, which is always true. So no restriction.

But we also need that the points are covered. The condition we derived is necessary but not sufficient.

We can try to find a construction for each n and k. So the pattern is: For a given n and k, we can use non-sunny lines to cover the largest n-k diagonals (if n-k is large enough) and then the sunny lines cover the remaining triangle of side k. But we also have the possibility to use non-sunny lines that are not all diagonals, leaving a triangle of side t that can be covered by t sunny lines. The triangle of side t can be covered by t sunny lines only if t = 1 or t = 3? But we also have t=4? For n=10, we covered triangle of side 3 (6 points) with 4 sunny lines, which is more than t. So the condition is not that the number of sunny lines equals the side; it's that the sunny lines can cover the points of the triangle. So we need to find, for a given n and k, whether we can cover the triangle of side n (points with sums 2 to n+1) with n lines, exactly k of which are sunny. This is a covering problem.

We can think of the lines as covering the points. The non-sunny lines can be chosen as diagonals covering the largest diagonals. The remaining points are those on the first n-t diagonals, where t = number of non-sunny lines. The number of sunny lines is k = n - t. So we need to cover the triangle of side n-t with n-t sunny lines.
